<p><b>James Madison, Jr.,</b> (March 16 [<a href="/wiki/Old_Style_and_New_Style_dates" title="Old Style and New Style dates">O.S.</a> March 5],&#160;1751&#160;– June 28, 1836) was an <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">American</a> statesman and <a href="/wiki/Founding_Fathers_of_the_United_States" title="Founding Fathers of the United States">Founding Father</a> who served as the <a href="/wiki/List_of_Presidents_of_the_United_States" title="List of Presidents of the United States">fourth President of the United States</a> from 1809 to 1817. He is hailed as the "Father of the Constitution" for his pivotal role in drafting and promoting the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Constitution" title="United States Constitution">United States Constitution</a> and the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Bill_of_Rights" title="United States Bill of Rights">Bill of Rights</a>.</p>
<p>Madison inherited his plantation <a href="/wiki/Montpelier_(Orange,_Virginia)" title="Montpelier (Orange, Virginia)">Montpelier</a> in Virginia and therewith owned hundreds of <a href="/wiki/Slaves" class="mw-redirect" title="Slaves">slaves</a> during his lifetime. He served as both a member of the Virginia House of Delegates and as a member of the <a href="/wiki/Continental_Congress" title="Continental Congress">Continental Congress</a> prior to the <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia_Convention" class="mw-redirect" title="Philadelphia Convention">Constitutional Convention</a>. After the Convention, he became one of the leaders in the movement to ratify the Constitution, both in Virginia and nationally. His collaboration with <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Hamilton" title="Alexander Hamilton">Alexander Hamilton</a> and <a href="/wiki/John_Jay" title="John Jay">John Jay</a> produced <a href="/wiki/The_Federalist_Papers" title="The Federalist Papers"><i>The Federalist</i> Papers</a>, among the most important treatises in support of the Constitution. Madison's political views changed throughout his life. During deliberations on the Constitution, he favored a strong national government, but later preferred stronger state governments, before settling between the two extremes later in his life.</p>
<p>In 1789, Madison became a leader in the new <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_of_Representatives" title="United States House of Representatives">House of Representatives</a>, drafting many general laws. He is noted for drafting the first ten amendments to the Constitution, and thus is known also as the "Father of the Bill of Rights." He worked closely with President <a href="/wiki/George_Washington" title="George Washington">George Washington</a> to organize the new federal government. Breaking with Hamilton and the <a href="/wiki/Federalist_Party" title="Federalist Party">Federalist Party</a> in 1791, he and <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson" title="Thomas Jefferson">Thomas Jefferson</a> organized the <a href="/wiki/Democratic-Republican_Party" title="Democratic-Republican Party">Democratic-Republican Party</a>. In response to the <a href="/wiki/Alien_and_Sedition_Acts" title="Alien and Sedition Acts">Alien and Sedition Acts</a>, Jefferson and Madison drafted the <a href="/wiki/Kentucky_and_Virginia_Resolutions" title="Kentucky and Virginia Resolutions">Kentucky and Virginia Resolutions</a>, arguing that states can <a href="/wiki/Nullification_(U.S._Constitution)" title="Nullification (U.S. Constitution)">nullify</a> unconstitutional laws.</p>
<p>As Jefferson's <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_State" title="United States Secretary of State">Secretary of State</a> (1801–1809), Madison supervised the <a href="/wiki/Louisiana_Purchase" title="Louisiana Purchase">Louisiana Purchase</a>, which doubled the nation's size. Madison succeeded Jefferson as president in 1809, was re-elected in 1813, and presided over renewed prosperity for several years. After the failure of diplomatic protests and a trade embargo against the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_of_Great_Britain_and_Ireland" title="United Kingdom of Great Britain and Ireland">United Kingdom</a>, he led the U.S. into the <a href="/wiki/War_of_1812" title="War of 1812">War of 1812</a>. The war was an administrative morass, as the United States had neither a strong army nor financial system. As a result, Madison afterward supported a stronger national government and military, as well as the <a href="/wiki/Second_Bank_of_the_United_States" title="Second Bank of the United States">national bank</a>, which he had long opposed. Madison has been <a href="/wiki/Historical_rankings_of_presidents_of_the_United_States" title="Historical rankings of presidents of the United States">ranked</a> in the aggregate by historians as the ninth most successful president.</p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life_and_education">Early life and education</span></h2>
<p>James Madison, Jr., was born on March 16, 1751, (March 5, 1751, <a href="/wiki/Old_Style_and_New_Style_dates" title="Old Style and New Style dates">Old Style</a>, <a href="/wiki/Julian_calendar" title="Julian calendar">Julian calendar</a>) at <a href="/wiki/Belle_Grove_(Port_Conway,_Virginia)" title="Belle Grove (Port Conway, Virginia)">Belle Grove Plantation</a> near <a href="/wiki/Port_Conway,_Virginia" title="Port Conway, Virginia">Port Conway, Virginia</a>, to father <a href="/wiki/James_Madison,_Sr." class="mw-redirect" title="James Madison, Sr.">James Madison, Sr.</a>, and mother <a href="/wiki/Nelly_Conway_Madison" class="mw-redirect" title="Nelly Conway Madison">Nelly Conway Madison</a>. He grew up as the oldest of twelve children.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> Nelly and James Sr. had seven more boys and four girls. Three of James Jr.'s brothers died as infants, including one who was <a href="/wiki/Stillbirth" title="Stillbirth">stillborn</a>. In the summer of 1775, his sister Elizabeth (age 7) and his brother Reuben (age 3) died from a <a href="/wiki/Dysentery" title="Dysentery">dysentery</a> epidemic that swept through <a href="/wiki/Orange_County,_Virginia" title="Orange County, Virginia">Orange County</a> because of contaminated water.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> His father, <a href="/wiki/James_Madison,_Sr." class="mw-redirect" title="James Madison, Sr.">James Madison, Sr.</a> (1723–1801), was a tobacco planter who grew up on a plantation, then called <a href="/wiki/Montpelier_(James_Madison)" class="mw-redirect" title="Montpelier (James Madison)">Mount Pleasant</a>, which he had inherited upon reaching adulthood. He later acquired more property and slaves, and with 5,000 acres (2,000&#160;ha), he became the largest landowner and a leading citizen in the Piedmont. James Jr.'s mother, <a href="/wiki/Nelly_Conway_Madison" class="mw-redirect" title="Nelly Conway Madison">Nelly Conway Madison</a> (1731–1829), was born at Port Conway, the daughter of a prominent planter and tobacco merchant.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> In these years, the southern colonies were becoming a slave society, in which slave labor powered the economy and slaveholders formed the political elite.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup></p>
<p>From age 11 to 16, young "Jemmy" Madison was sent to study under Donald Robertson, an instructor at the Innes Plantation in <a href="/wiki/King_and_Queen_County,_Virginia" title="King and Queen County, Virginia">King and Queen County, Virginia</a>, in the <a href="/wiki/Tidewater_region_of_Virginia" class="mw-redirect" title="Tidewater region of Virginia">Tidewater</a> region. Robertson was a Scottish teacher who tutored a number of prominent plantation families in the South. From Robertson, Madison learned mathematics, geography, and modern and classical languages—he became especially proficient in Latin. He attributed his instinct for learning "largely to that man [Robertson]."<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup></p>
<p>At age 16, he returned to <a href="/wiki/Montpelier_(Orange,_Virginia)" title="Montpelier (Orange, Virginia)">Montpelier</a>, where he began a two-year course of study under the Reverend Thomas Martin in preparation for college. Unlike most college-bound Virginians of his day, Madison eschewed the <a href="/wiki/College_of_William_and_Mary" class="mw-redirect" title="College of William and Mary">College of William and Mary</a>, where the lowland <a href="/wiki/Williamsburg,_Virginia" title="Williamsburg, Virginia">Williamsburg</a> climate—more susceptible to infectious disease—might have strained his delicate health. Instead, in 1769, he enrolled at the College of New Jersey, now <a href="/wiki/Princeton_University" title="Princeton University">Princeton University</a>, where he became roommates and close friends with poet <a href="/wiki/Philip_Freneau" title="Philip Freneau">Philip Freneau</a>. Madison proposed in vain to Freneau's sister Mary.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:177px;"><a href="/wiki/File:1811,_Sharples,_James,_James_Madison.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/1811%2C_Sharples%2C_James%2C_James_Madison.jpg/175px-1811%2C_Sharples%2C_James%2C_James_Madison.jpg" width="175" height="214" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/1811%2C_Sharples%2C_James%2C_James_Madison.jpg/263px-1811%2C_Sharples%2C_James%2C_James_Madison.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/1811%2C_Sharples%2C_James%2C_James_Madison.jpg/350px-1811%2C_Sharples%2C_James%2C_James_Madison.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1633" data-file-height="2000" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:1811,_Sharples,_James,_James_Madison.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Madison at Princeton University. Portrait by <a href="/wiki/James_Sharples" title="James Sharples">James Sharples</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Through diligence and long hours of study that may have compromised his health,<sup id="cite_ref-brennan_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-brennan-9">[9]</a></sup> Madison graduated in 1771. His studies included <a href="/wiki/Classical_Latin" title="Classical Latin">Latin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">Greek</a>, <a href="/wiki/Science" title="Science">science</a>, <a href="/wiki/Geography" title="Geography">geography</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mathematics" title="Mathematics">mathematics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rhetoric" title="Rhetoric">rhetoric</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a>. Great emphasis was placed on speech and debate also; Madison helped found the <a href="/wiki/American_Whig%E2%80%93Cliosophic_Society" title="American Whig–Cliosophic Society">American Whig Society</a>, in direct competition to fellow student <a href="/wiki/Aaron_Burr" title="Aaron Burr">Aaron Burr</a>'s Cliosophic Society. After graduation, Madison remained at Princeton to study <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_language" title="Hebrew language">Hebrew</a> and <a href="/wiki/Political_philosophy" title="Political philosophy">political philosophy</a> under the university president, <a href="/wiki/John_Witherspoon" title="John Witherspoon">John Witherspoon</a>, before returning to Montpelier in the spring of 1772. He became quite fluent in <a href="/wiki/List_of_Presidents_of_the_United_States_by_languages_spoken" class="mw-redirect" title="List of Presidents of the United States by languages spoken">Hebrew</a>. Madison studied <a href="/wiki/Law" title="Law">law</a> from his interest in public policy rather than the intent to practice law.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">Religion</span></h3>
<p>Although educated by <a href="/wiki/Presbyterianism" title="Presbyterianism">Presbyterian</a> clergymen, young Madison was an avid reader of English <a href="/wiki/Deism" title="Deism">deist</a> tracts.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> Madison as an adult paid little attention to religious matters. Biographer Hutson noted that historians generally find that after he left college, "there [was] no trace, no clue as to his personal religious convictions."<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> However, some scholars indicate he leaned toward <a href="/wiki/Deism" title="Deism">deism</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> Others maintain that Madison accepted Christian tenets and formed his outlook on life with a Christian world view.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Military_service_and_early_political_career">Military service and early political career</span></h2>
<p>After graduation from Princeton, Madison became interested in the relationship between the American colonies and Great Britain, which deteriorated over the issue of British taxation. In 1774, Madison took a seat on the local Committee of Safety, a patriot pro-revolution group that oversaw the local militia. This was the first step in a life of public service that his family's wealth facilitated.<sup id="cite_ref-Stagg_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stagg-16">[16]</a></sup> In October 1775, at the start of the <a href="/wiki/American_Revolutionary_War" title="American Revolutionary War">American Revolutionary War</a>, he was commissioned as the colonel of the <a href="/wiki/Orange_County,_Virginia" title="Orange County, Virginia">Orange County</a> militia, serving as his father's second-in-command until his election as a delegate to the <a href="/wiki/Fifth_Virginia_Convention" title="Fifth Virginia Convention">Fifth Virginia Convention</a>, which produced Virginia's first <a href="/wiki/Constitution" title="Constitution">constitution</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:James_Madison,_by_Charles_Willson_Peale,_1783.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/James_Madison%2C_by_Charles_Willson_Peale%2C_1783.png/170px-James_Madison%2C_by_Charles_Willson_Peale%2C_1783.png" width="170" height="227" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/James_Madison%2C_by_Charles_Willson_Peale%2C_1783.png/255px-James_Madison%2C_by_Charles_Willson_Peale%2C_1783.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/James_Madison%2C_by_Charles_Willson_Peale%2C_1783.png/340px-James_Madison%2C_by_Charles_Willson_Peale%2C_1783.png 2x" data-file-width="1272" data-file-height="1700" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:James_Madison,_by_Charles_Willson_Peale,_1783.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Congressman Madison, age 32 by <a href="/wiki/Charles_Willson_Peale" title="Charles Willson Peale">Charles Willson Peale</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>At the Virginia constitutional convention, Madison supported the <a href="/wiki/Virginia_Declaration_of_Rights" title="Virginia Declaration of Rights">Virginia Declaration of Rights</a>, though he argued that it should contain stronger protections for <a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_religion" title="Freedom of religion">freedom of religion</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> He had earlier witnessed the persecution of <a href="/wiki/Southern_Baptist_Convention#History" title="Southern Baptist Convention">Baptist</a> preachers in Virginia, who were arrested for preaching without a license from the established <a href="/wiki/Anglican_Church" class="mw-redirect" title="Anglican Church">Anglican Church</a>. He collaborated with the Baptist preacher <a href="/wiki/Elijah_Craig" title="Elijah Craig">Elijah Craig</a> to promote constitutional guarantees for religious liberty in Virginia.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> Madison and <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson" title="Thomas Jefferson">Thomas Jefferson</a>, who became a mentor to Madison, drafted the <a href="/wiki/Virginia_Statute_for_Religious_Freedom" title="Virginia Statute for Religious Freedom">Virginia Statute for Religious Freedom</a>, which was finally passed in 1786.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup></p>
<p>Madison served on the Virginia governor's Council of State from 1777 to 1779, at which point he was elected to the <a href="/wiki/Congress_of_the_Confederation" title="Congress of the Confederation">Congress of the Confederation</a>. The country faced a difficult war against <a href="/wiki/Great_Britain" title="Great Britain">Great Britain</a>, as well as runaway <a href="/wiki/Inflation" title="Inflation">inflation</a>, financial troubles, and lack of cooperation between the different levels of government. Madison worked to make himself an expert on financial issues, becoming deeply involved in congressional committees, becoming a legislative workhorse and a master of parliamentary coalition building.<sup id="cite_ref-Stagg_16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stagg-16">[16]</a></sup> Frustrated by the failure of the states to supply needed requisitions, Madison proposed a standing army, a permanent navy, and the ability of Congress to compel states to meet their war-time obligations. Madison also persuaded Virginia to give up its claims to northwestern territories—consisting of most of modern-day <a href="/wiki/Ohio" title="Ohio">Ohio</a> and points west—to the Continental Congress.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> After serving Congress from 1781 to 1783, Madison won election to the <a href="/wiki/Virginia_House_of_Delegates" title="Virginia House of Delegates">Virginia House of Delegates</a> in 1784.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup></p>
<p>Madison served in the Virginia House of Delegates from 1784 to 1786. During these years in the House of Delegates, Madison grew increasingly frustrated with what he saw as excessive democracy. He criticized the tendency for delegates to cater to the particular interests of their constituents, even if such interests were destructive to the state at large. In particular, he was troubled by a law that denied diplomatic immunity to ambassadors from other countries, and a law that legalized paper money.<sup id="cite_ref-Wood1_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wood1-22">[22]</a></sup> He thought legislators should be "disinterested" and act in the interests of their state at large, even if this contradicted the wishes of constituents. Madison believed this "excessive democracy" was the cause of a larger social decay which he and others (such as Washington) thought had resumed after the revolution and was nearing a tipping point—<a href="/wiki/Shays%27_Rebellion" title="Shays' Rebellion">Shays' Rebellion</a> was an example.<sup id="cite_ref-Wood1_22-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wood1-22">[22]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Father_of_the_Constitution">Father of the Constitution</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia_Convention" class="mw-redirect" title="Philadelphia Convention">Philadelphia Convention</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Constitution_of_the_United_States,_page_1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Constitution_of_the_United_States%2C_page_1.jpg/220px-Constitution_of_the_United_States%2C_page_1.jpg" width="220" height="266" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Constitution_of_the_United_States%2C_page_1.jpg/330px-Constitution_of_the_United_States%2C_page_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Constitution_of_the_United_States%2C_page_1.jpg/440px-Constitution_of_the_United_States%2C_page_1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="7258" data-file-height="8785" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Constitution_of_the_United_States,_page_1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<center>Page one of the original copy<br />
of the <a href="/wiki/U.S._Constitution" class="mw-redirect" title="U.S. Constitution">U.S. Constitution</a></center>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Articles_of_Confederation" title="Articles of Confederation">Articles of Confederation</a> established the United States as an association of sovereign states with a weak central government. This arrangement was met with disapproval, and was mostly unsuccessful after the war. Congress had no power to tax, and was unable to pay debts from the Revolution, which concerned Madison and other nationalists, such as Washington and <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Hamilton" title="Alexander Hamilton">Alexander Hamilton</a>, who feared national bankruptcy and disunion.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> The historian <a href="/wiki/Gordon_S._Wood" title="Gordon S. Wood">Gordon S. Wood</a> has noted that many leaders, including Madison and Washington, feared more that the revolution had not fixed the social problems that had triggered it and that the excesses ascribed to the King were being seen in the state legislatures. <a href="/wiki/Shays%27_Rebellion" title="Shays' Rebellion">Shays' Rebellion</a> is often cited as the event that forced the issue; Wood argues that many at the time saw it as only the most extreme example of democratic excess.<sup id="cite_ref-Wood1_22-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wood1-22">[22]</a></sup></p>
<p>As Madison wrote, "a crisis had arrived which was to decide whether the American experiment was to be a blessing to the world, or to blast for ever the hopes which the republican cause had inspired."<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> Madison initially hoped to amend the Articles of Confederation, and at the 1786 <a href="/wiki/Annapolis_Convention_(1786)" title="Annapolis Convention (1786)">Annapolis Convention</a>, he supported the calling of another convention to consider amending the Articles. After winning election to another term in the Congress of the Confederation, Madison helped convince the other Congressmen to authorize the <a href="/wiki/Constitutional_Convention_(United_States)" title="Constitutional Convention (United States)">Philadelphia convention</a> for the purposes of proposing new amendments. But Madison had come to believe that the ineffectual Articles had to be superseded by a new constitution, and he began preparing for a convention that would do more than merely propose amendments. Madison was pivotal in persuading Washington to attend the 1787 convention—he knew how instrumental the general would be to the adoption of a new constitution.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup></p>
<p>Years earlier Madison had pored over crates of books that Jefferson sent him from <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a> on various forms of government. The historian Douglas Adair called Madison's work "probably the most fruitful piece of scholarly research ever carried out by an American."<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> As a quorum was being reached for the convention to begin, the 36-year-old Madison wrote what became known as the <a href="/wiki/Virginia_Plan" title="Virginia Plan">Virginia Plan</a>, an outline for a new constitution.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> Many delegates were surprised to learn that the plan called for the abrogation of the Articles and the creation of a new constitution, to be ratified by special conventions in each state rather than the state legislatures. Nonetheless, with the assent of prominent attendees such as Washington and <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Franklin" title="Benjamin Franklin">Benjamin Franklin</a>, the delegates went into a secret session to consider the creation of a new constitution.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> Though the Virginia Plan was an outline rather than a draft of a possible constitution, and though it was extensively changed during the debate (especially by <a href="/wiki/John_Rutledge" title="John Rutledge">John Rutledge</a> and <a href="/wiki/James_Wilson" title="James Wilson">James Wilson</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Committee_of_Detail" title="Committee of Detail">Committee of Detail</a>), its use at the convention led many to call Madison the "Father of the Constitution".<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup></p>
<p>During the course of the Convention, Madison spoke over two hundred times, and his fellow delegates rated him highly. William Pierce wrote that "... every Person seems to acknowledge his greatness. In the management of every great question he evidently took the lead in the Convention ... he always comes forward as the best informed Man of any point in debate." Madison recorded the unofficial minutes of the convention, and these have become the only comprehensive record of what occurred. The historian <a href="/wiki/Clinton_Rossiter" title="Clinton Rossiter">Clinton Rossiter</a> regarded Madison's performance as "a combination of learning, experience, purpose, and imagination that not even Adams or Jefferson could have equaled."<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Gordon_S._Wood" title="Gordon S. Wood">Gordon Wood</a> argues Madison's frustrating experience in the Virginia legislature years earlier most shaped his constitutional views. Wood notes that the governmental structure in both the Virginia Plan and the final constitution were not innovative, since they were copied from the British government, had been used in the states since 1776, and numerous authors had already argued for their adoption at the national level.<sup id="cite_ref-WoodGordon_a_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WoodGordon_a-31">[31]</a></sup> The controversial elements in the Virginia Plan were removed, and the rest was commonly accepted as necessary for a functional government (state or national) for decades, hence Madison's contribution was considered more qualitative.<sup id="cite_ref-WoodGordon_a_31-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WoodGordon_a-31">[31]</a></sup> Wood argues that, like most national politicians of the late 1780s, Madison believed that the problem was less with the Articles of Confederation than with the nature of the state legislatures. He believed the solution was to restrain the excesses of the states. This required more than an alteration in the Articles of Confederation; it required a change in the character of the national compact. The ultimate question before the convention, Wood notes, was not how to design a government but whether the states should remain sovereign, whether sovereignty should be transferred to the national government, or whether the constitution should settle somewhere in between.<sup id="cite_ref-WoodGordon_a_31-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WoodGordon_a-31">[31]</a></sup></p>
<p>Those, like Madison, who thought democracy in the state legislatures was excessive and insufficiently "disinterested", wanted sovereignty transferred to the national government, while those (like <a href="/wiki/Patrick_Henry" title="Patrick Henry">Patrick Henry</a>) who did not think this a problem, wanted to fix the Articles of Confederation. Madison was among the few delegates who wanted to deprive the states of sovereignty completely, which he considered the only solution to the problem. Though sharing the same goal as Madison, most other delegates reacted strongly against such an extreme change to the <i>status quo.</i> Though Madison lost most of his battles over how to amend the Virginia Plan (most importantly over the exclusion of the <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Revision" title="Council of Revision">Council of Revision</a>), in the process he increasingly shifted the debate away from a position of pure state sovereignty. Since most disagreements over what to include in the constitution were ultimately disputes over the balance of sovereignty between the states and national government, Madison's influence was critical. Wood notes that Madison's ultimate contribution was not in designing any particular constitutional framework, but in shifting the debate toward a compromise of "shared sovereignty" between the national and state governments.<sup id="cite_ref-WoodGordon_a_31-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WoodGordon_a-31">[31]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Federalist_Papers_and_ratification_debates"><i>The Federalist</i> Papers and ratification debates</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/The_Federalist_Papers" title="The Federalist Papers">The Federalist Papers</a></div>
<p>Following the Constitutional Convention, there ensued an intense battle over the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Constitution" title="United States Constitution">Constitution's</a> <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_United_States_Constitution#Ratification_of_the_Constitution" title="History of the United States Constitution">ratification</a>. Each state was requested to hold a special convention to deliberate and determine whether or not to ratify the Constitution.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> Madison returned to New York, where the Confederation Congress was in session.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> There he was approached by Alexander Hamilton, who asked him to help write the <i>The Federalist Papers</i>, a series of 85 newspaper articles published in New York that explained and defended the proposed Constitution. Under the pseudonym <i>Publius</i>, Hamilton, Madison, and <a href="/wiki/John_Jay" title="John Jay">John Jay</a> wrote 85 essays in the span of six months, with Madison writing 29 of the essays. The articles were also published in book form and became a virtual debater's handbook for the supporters of the Constitution in the ratifying conventions. Historian Clinton Rossiter called <i>The Federalist</i> Papers "the most important work in political science that ever has been written, or is likely ever to be written, in the United States."<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Federalist_No._10" title="Federalist No. 10">Federalist No. 10</a>, Madison's first contribution the papers, became highly-regarded in the 20th century for its advocacy for <a href="/wiki/Representative_democracy" title="Representative democracy">representative democracy</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup></p>
<p>Consensus held that if Virginia, the most populous state at the time, did not ratify the Constitution, the new national government would not likely succeed. When the <a href="/wiki/Virginia_Ratifying_Convention" title="Virginia Ratifying Convention">Virginia Ratifying Convention</a> began on June 2, 1788, the Constitution had not yet been ratified by the required nine states. New York, the second largest state and a bastion of anti-federalism, would likely not ratify it without Virginia, and Virginia's exclusion from the new government would disqualify Virginian George Washington from being the first president.<sup id="cite_ref-LabunskiRichard_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LabunskiRichard-37">[37]</a></sup> Virginia delegates believed that Washington's election as the first president was a condition for their acceptance of the new constitution and the new government. Arguably the most prominent anti-federalist, the powerful orator <a href="/wiki/Patrick_Henry" title="Patrick Henry">Patrick Henry</a> was a delegate and had a following second only to Washington (who was not a delegate). Most delegates believed that Virginians mainly opposed the constitution.<sup id="cite_ref-LabunskiRichard_37-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LabunskiRichard-37">[37]</a></sup> Initially Madison did not want to stand for election to the Virginia ratifying convention, but was persuaded to do so because the situation looked so bad. His role at the convention was likely critical to Virginia's ratification, and thus to the success of the constitution generally.<sup id="cite_ref-LabunskiRichard_37-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LabunskiRichard-37">[37]</a></sup></p>
<p>Although Henry was by far the more powerful and dramatic speaker, Madison successfully matched him. Madison's expertise on the subject he had long argued for allowed him to respond with rational arguments to Henry's emotional appeals.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> Madison persuaded prominent figures such as <a href="/wiki/Edmund_Randolph" title="Edmund Randolph">Edmund Randolph</a>, who had refused to endorse the constitution at the convention, to change their position and support it at the ratifying convention. Randolph's switch likely changed the votes of several more anti-federalists.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> When the vote was nearing, and the constitution appeared headed for defeat, Madison pleaded with a small group of anti-federalists, and promised them that, if they changed their votes, he would later push for a bill of rights to provide limits to the power of the new government.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>A resolution was proposed that a declaration of rights be referred to the other States in the American Confederation for their consideration prior to ratification of the constitution.<sup id="cite_ref-Grigsby1_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Grigsby1-40">[40]</a></sup> Supported by <a href="/wiki/George_Mason#Ratification_battle" title="George Mason">George Mason</a> and Patrick Henry but opposed by Madison, <a href="/wiki/Henry_Lee_III" title="Henry Lee III">Henry Lee III (Light-Horse Harry Lee)</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Marshall" title="John Marshall">John Marshall</a>, Randolph, and <a href="/wiki/Bushrod_Washington" title="Bushrod Washington">Bushrod Washington</a>, the resolution failed, 88–80.<sup id="cite_ref-Grigsby1_40-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Grigsby1-40">[40]</a></sup> Lee, Madison, Marshall, Randolph, and Washington then voted in favor of a resolution to ratify the constitution, which the convention approved on June 28, 1789 by a vote of 89–79, with Mason and Henry voting in the minority.<sup id="cite_ref-Grigsby1_40-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Grigsby1-40">[40]</a></sup></p>
<p>Addressing slavery in the Constitution, Madison set out with a view that <a href="/wiki/African_American" class="mw-redirect" title="African American">African American</a> slaves as an "unfortunate race" and believed their true nature was both human and property.<sup id="cite_ref-Willspp276-278_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Willspp276-278-41">[41]</a></sup> On February 12, 1788, Madison, in the <a href="/wiki/Federalist_No._54" title="Federalist No. 54">Federalist Letter No. 54</a>, stated that the Constitutional <a href="/wiki/Three-fifths_compromise" class="mw-redirect" title="Three-fifths compromise">three-fifths compromise</a> clause was the best alternative for the slaves' current condition and for determining representation of citizens in Congress.<sup id="cite_ref-Willspp276-278_41-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Willspp276-278-41">[41]</a></sup> Madison believed that slaves, as property, would be protected by both their masters and the government.<sup id="cite_ref-Willspp276-278_41-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Willspp276-278-41">[41]</a></sup></p>
<p>Madison was called the "Father of the Constitution" by his peers in his lifetime. However, he was modest, and he protested the title as being "a credit to which I have no claim. ... The Constitution was not, like the fabled Goddess of Wisdom, the offspring of a single brain. It ought to be regarded as the work of many heads and many hands".<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> He wrote Hamilton at the New York ratifying convention, stating his opinion that "ratification was <i>in toto</i> and 'for ever'".<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Member_of_Congress">Member of Congress</span></h2>
<p>After Virginia ratified the constitution, Madison returned to New York to resume his duties in the Congress of the Confederation. At the request of Washington, Madison sought a seat in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senate" title="United States Senate">United States Senate</a>, but his election was blocked by Patrick Henry. Madison then decided to run for a seat in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_of_Representatives" title="United States House of Representatives">United States House of Representatives</a>. At Henry's behest, the Virginia legislature <a href="/wiki/Gerrymandering_in_the_United_States" title="Gerrymandering in the United States">created congressional districts</a> designed to deny Madison a seat, and Henry recruited a strong challenger to Madison in <a href="/wiki/James_Monroe" title="James Monroe">James Monroe</a>. Locked in a difficult race against Monroe, Madison promised to support a series of constitutional amendments to protect individual liberties.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> Madison's promise paid off, as he won election to Congress with 57% of the vote.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> He would continue to serve in the House until 1797, when Madison briefly left public office to preside over his plantation.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> Early in his tenure, Madison became an ally of President Washington, who looked to Madison as the person who best understood the constitution.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Father_of_the_Bill_of_Rights">Father of the Bill of Rights</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/United_States_Bill_of_Rights" title="United States Bill of Rights">United States Bill of Rights</a></div>
<p>Though the idea for a bill of rights had been suggested at the end of the constitutional convention, the delegates wanted to go home and thought the proposal unnecessary. The omission of a bill of rights became the main argument of the anti-federalists against the constitution. Though no state conditioned ratification of the constitution on a bill of rights, several states came close, and the issue almost prevented the constitution from being ratified. Some anti-federalists continued to contest this after the constitution was ratified and threatened the entire nation with another, more partisan, constitutional convention. Madison objected to a specific bill of rights for several reasons:<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> he thought it was unnecessary, since it purported to protect against powers that the federal government had not been granted; he also considered the enumeration of some rights might be taken to imply the absence of other rights; and that at the state level, bills of rights had proven to be useless paper barriers against government powers.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup></p>
<p>Though few in the new congress wanted to debate a possible Bill of Rights (for the next century, most thought that the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Declaration_of_Independence" title="United States Declaration of Independence">Declaration of Independence</a>, not the first ten constitutional amendments, constituted the true Bill of Rights), Madison pressed the issue.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> Congress was busy setting up the new government, most wanted to wait for the system to show its defects before amending the constitution, and the anti-federalist movements (which had demanded a new convention) had died out quickly once the constitution was ratified. Despite this, Madison still feared that the states would compel congress to call for a new constitutional convention, which they had the right to do. He also believed that the constitution did not sufficiently protect the national government from excessive democracy and parochialism (the defects he saw in the state governments), so he saw the amendments as mitigation of these problems. On June 8, 1789, Madison introduced his bill proposing amendments consisting of Nine Articles comprising up to 20 potential amendments. Madison initially proposed that the amendments be incorporated into the body of the Constitution. The House passed most of the amendments, but rejected the idea of placing them in the body of the Constitution. Instead, it adopted 17 amendments to be attached separately and sent this bill to the Senate.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup></p>
<p>Madison was a staunch proponent of the right of Americans to a civil <a href="/wiki/Jury_trial" title="Jury trial">jury trial</a>. He initially introduced an amendment to the U.S. Constitution that guaranteed all citizens the right to a jury trial in all civil cases where there was $20 or more at stake. While the original amendment failed, the guaranty of a civil jury trial was incorporated into the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Bill_of_Rights" title="United States Bill of Rights">Bill of Rights</a> as the <a href="/wiki/Seventh_Amendment_to_the_United_States_Constitution" title="Seventh Amendment to the United States Constitution">Seventh Amendment to the United States Constitution</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> In June 1789, urging adoption of the right to civil jury trials, he explained, "Trial by jury in civil cases is as essential to secure the liberty of the people as any one of the pre-existent rights of nature."<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Senate edited the amendments still further, making 26 changes of its own, and condensing their number to twelve.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> Madison's proposal to apply parts of the Bill of Rights to the states as well as the federal government was eliminated, as was his final proposed change to the preamble.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> A House–Senate <a href="/wiki/United_States_congressional_conference_committee" title="United States congressional conference committee">Conference Committee</a> then convened to resolve the numerous differences between the two Bill of Rights proposals. On September 24, 1789, the committee issued its report, which finalized 12 Constitutional Amendments for the House and Senate to consider. This version was approved by <a href="/wiki/Joint_resolution" title="Joint resolution">joint resolution</a> of Congress on September 25, 1789.<sup id="cite_ref-adamson2008p93_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-adamson2008p93-58">[58]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup></p>
<p>Articles Three through Twelve were ratified as additions to the Constitution December 15, 1791, and became the Bill of Rights.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> Article Two became part of the Constitution in 1992 as the <a href="/wiki/Twenty-seventh_Amendment_to_the_United_States_Constitution" title="Twenty-seventh Amendment to the United States Constitution">Twenty-seventh Amendment</a>. <a href="/wiki/Congressional_Apportionment_Amendment" title="Congressional Apportionment Amendment">Article One</a> is technically still pending before the states.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Debates_on_foreign_policy">Debates on foreign policy</span></h3>
<p>When Britain and France went to war in 1793, the U.S. was caught in the middle. The 1778 treaty of alliance with France was still in effect, yet most of the new country's trade was with Britain. War with Britain became imminent in 1794, after the British seized hundreds of American ships that were trading with French colonies. Madison believed that the United States was stronger than Britain, and that a trade war with Britain, although risking a real war by that government, would probably succeed, and allow Americans to assert their independence fully. Great Britain, he charged, "has bound us in commercial manacles, and very nearly defeated the object of our independence." According to Varg, Madison discounted the more powerful British military when the latter declared "her interests can be wounded almost mortally, while ours are invulnerable." The British West Indies, Madison maintained, could not live without American foodstuffs, but Americans could easily do without British manufactures. He concluded, "it is in our power, in a very short time, to supply all the tonnage necessary for our own commerce".<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> Washington avoided a trade war and instead secured friendly trade relations with Britain through the <a href="/wiki/Jay_Treaty" title="Jay Treaty">Jay Treaty</a> of 1794. Madison's harsh and unsuccessful opposition to the treaty led to a permanent break with Washington, ending a long friendship. The debate over the Jay Treaty would serve as the catalyst for the creation of the country's first political parties.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Founding_the_Democratic-Republican_party">Founding the Democratic-Republican party</span></h3>
<p>Supporters for ratification of the Constitution became known as the <a href="/wiki/Federalist_Party" title="Federalist Party">Federalist Party</a>. Those in opposition were labeled <a href="/wiki/Anti-Federalist" class="mw-redirect" title="Anti-Federalist">Anti-Federalists</a>, but neither group was a <a href="/wiki/Political_party" title="Political party">political party</a> in the modern sense. Following ratification and formation of the first government in 1789, two new political factions formed along similar lines as the old division. The supporters of <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Hamilton" title="Alexander Hamilton">Alexander Hamilton</a>'s attempts to strengthen the national government called themselves Federalists, while those who opposed Hamilton called themselves "Republicans" (later historians would refer to them as the <a href="/wiki/Democratic-Republican_Party" title="Democratic-Republican Party">Democratic-Republican Party</a>). Madison and other Republican Party organizers, who favored <a href="/wiki/States%27_rights" title="States' rights">states' rights</a> and local control, were struggling to find an institutional solution to the Constitution's seeming inability to prevent concentration of power in an <a href="/wiki/Administrative_republic" class="mw-redirect" title="Administrative republic">administrative republic</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> As first Secretary of the Treasury, Hamilton created many new federal institutions, including the <a href="/wiki/First_Bank_of_the_United_States" title="First Bank of the United States">Bank of the United States</a>. Madison led the unsuccessful attempt in Congress to block Hamilton's proposal, arguing that the new Constitution did not explicitly allow the federal government to form a bank. As early as May 26, 1792, Hamilton complained, "Mr. Madison cooperating with Mr. Jefferson is at the head of a faction decidedly hostile to me and my administration."<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> As Hamilton's influence in the administration grew, Madison and Jefferson increasingly came to fear the possible restoration of a monarchy, and they worked together to build an opposition to Hamilton's policies. By the end of Washington's term, most Congressmen identified as either Democratic-Republicans or Federalists.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup></p>
<p>Washington and Hamilton both left the government after 1797, but Madison and Jefferson remained in opposition during the Federalist presidency of <a href="/wiki/John_Adams" title="John Adams">John Adams</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> In 1798, the U.S. and France unofficially became combatants in the <a href="/wiki/Quasi_War" class="mw-redirect" title="Quasi War">Quasi War</a>, that involved naval warships and commercial vessels battling in the Caribbean. The Federalists created a standing army and passed the <a href="/wiki/Alien_and_Sedition_Acts" title="Alien and Sedition Acts">Alien and Sedition Acts</a>, which were directed at French refugees engaged in American politics and against Republican editors. In response, Madison and Jefferson secretly drafted the <a href="/wiki/Virginia_and_Kentucky_Resolutions" class="mw-redirect" title="Virginia and Kentucky Resolutions">Virginia and Kentucky Resolutions</a> declaring the enactments to be unconstitutional and noted that "states, in contesting obnoxious laws, should 'interpose for arresting the progress of the evil.'"<sup id="cite_ref-Chernow1_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chernow1-68">[68]</a></sup> The resolutions were largely unpopular, even among republicans, since they called for state governments to invalidate federal laws. Jefferson went further, urging states to secede if necessary, though Madison convinced Jefferson to relent this extreme view.<sup id="cite_ref-Chernow1_68-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chernow1-68">[68]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to Chernow, Madison's position "was a breathtaking evolution for a man who had pleaded at the Constitutional Convention that the federal government should possess a veto over state laws." Chernow argues that Madison's politics remained closely aligned with Jefferson's until his experience as president with a weak national government during the War of 1812 caused Madison to appreciate the need for a strong central government to aid national defense. At the time, he began to support a national bank, a stronger navy, and a standing army.<sup id="cite_ref-Chernow1_68-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chernow1-68">[68]</a></sup></p>
<table class="infobox" style="width:22em;width: 210px; float: right; clear: right; margin:0 0 1.0em 1.0em">
<caption>External video</caption>
<tr>
<td colspan="2" style="text-align:center;text-align: left"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg/16px-Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg.png" width="16" height="16" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg/24px-Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg/32px-Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="128" data-file-height="128" /> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.c-span.org/video/?68744-1/sacred-fire-liberty-james-madison"><i>Booknotes</i> interview with Lance Banning on <i>The Sacred Fire of Liberty: James Madison and the Founding of the Federal Republic</i>, February 11, 1996</a>, <a href="/wiki/C-SPAN" title="C-SPAN">C-SPAN</a></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>The historian <a href="/wiki/Gordon_S._Wood" title="Gordon S. Wood">Gordon S. Wood</a> says that Lance Banning, as in his <i>Sacred Fire of Liberty</i> (1995), is the "only present-day scholar to maintain that Madison did not change his views in the 1790s."<sup id="cite_ref-Problem_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Problem-69">[69]</a></sup> In claiming this, Banning downplays Madison's nationalism in the 1780s.<sup id="cite_ref-Problem_69-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Problem-69">[69]</a></sup> Wood notes that many historians struggle to understand Madison, but Wood looks at him in the terms of Madison's own times—as a nationalist but one with a different conception of nationalism from that of the Federalists. He wanted to avoid a European-style government and always thought that the embargo would ultimately have been successful.<sup id="cite_ref-Problem_69-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Problem-69">[69]</a></sup> Thus, Wood assesses Madison from a different point of view.<sup id="cite_ref-Problem_69-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Problem-69">[69]</a></sup> Gary Rosen and Banning use other approaches to suggest Madison's consistency.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Marriage_and_family">Marriage and family</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:MadisonsMontpelier.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/MadisonsMontpelier.jpg/220px-MadisonsMontpelier.jpg" width="220" height="157" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/MadisonsMontpelier.jpg/330px-MadisonsMontpelier.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/MadisonsMontpelier.jpg/440px-MadisonsMontpelier.jpg 2x" data-file-width="750" data-file-height="535" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:MadisonsMontpelier.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Montpelier_(Orange,_Virginia)" title="Montpelier (Orange, Virginia)">Montpelier</a>, Madison's tobacco plantation in Virginia</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Madison was married for the first time at the age of 43; on September 15, 1794, James Madison married <a href="/wiki/Dolley_Payne_Todd" class="mw-redirect" title="Dolley Payne Todd">Dolley Payne Todd</a>, a 26-year-old widow, at <a href="/wiki/Harewood_(West_Virginia)" title="Harewood (West Virginia)">Harewood</a>, in what is now <a href="/wiki/Jefferson_County,_West_Virginia" title="Jefferson County, West Virginia">Jefferson County, West Virginia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> Madison had no children but adopted Todd's one surviving son, <a href="/wiki/John_Payne_Todd" title="John Payne Todd">John Payne Todd</a> (known as Payne), after the marriage.</p>
<p>Dolley Payne was born May 20, 1768, at the New Garden <a href="/wiki/Quaker" class="mw-redirect" title="Quaker">Quaker</a> settlement in North Carolina, where her parents, John Payne and Mary Coles Payne, lived briefly. Dolley's sister, <a href="/wiki/Lucy_Washington" title="Lucy Washington">Lucy Payne</a>, had recently married <a href="/wiki/George_Steptoe_Washington" title="George Steptoe Washington">George Steptoe Washington</a>, a nephew of President Washington. As a member of Congress, Madison had doubtless met the widow Todd at social functions in Philadelphia, then the nation's capital. She had been living there with her late husband. In May 1794, Madison asked their mutual friend <a href="/wiki/Aaron_Burr" title="Aaron Burr">Aaron Burr</a> to arrange a meeting. By August, she had accepted his proposal of marriage. For marrying Madison, a non-Quaker, she was expelled from the <a href="/wiki/Religious_Society_of_Friends" class="mw-redirect" title="Religious Society of Friends">Society of Friends</a>. Also in 1794 Madison was elected a Fellow of the <a href="/wiki/American_Academy_of_Arts_and_Sciences" title="American Academy of Arts and Sciences">American Academy of Arts and Sciences</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup></p>
<p>Dolley Madison put her social gifts to use when the couple lived in Washington, beginning when he was Secretary of State. With the White House still under construction, she advised as to its furnishings and sometimes served as First Lady for ceremonial functions for President Thomas Jefferson, a widower and friend. When her husband was president, she created the role of First Lady, using her social talents to advance his program. She is credited with adding to his popularity in office.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Madison's father died in 1801 and at age 50, Madison inherited the large plantation of Montpelier and other holdings, and his father's 108 slaves. He had begun to act as a steward of his father's properties by 1780.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="United_States_Secretary_of_State_1801.E2.80.931809">United States Secretary of State 1801–1809</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Louisiana_Purchase" title="Louisiana Purchase">Louisiana Purchase</a> and <a href="/wiki/Embargo_Act_of_1807" title="Embargo Act of 1807">Embargo Act of 1807</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Louisiana_purchase_treaty.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Louisiana_purchase_treaty.jpg/220px-Louisiana_purchase_treaty.jpg" width="220" height="329" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Louisiana_purchase_treaty.jpg/330px-Louisiana_purchase_treaty.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Louisiana_purchase_treaty.jpg/440px-Louisiana_purchase_treaty.jpg 2x" data-file-width="586" data-file-height="877" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Louisiana_purchase_treaty.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The original treaty of the Louisiana Purchase</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Jefferson defeated Adams in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1800" title="United States presidential election, 1800">1800 presidential election</a>. Jefferson wanted to oversee <a href="/wiki/Presidency_of_Thomas_Jefferson" title="Presidency of Thomas Jefferson">his administration</a>'s foreign policy, and he selected the loyal Madison for the position of Secretary of State despite the latter's lack of foreign policy experience. Along with Secretary of the Treasury <a href="/wiki/Albert_Gallatin" title="Albert Gallatin">Albert Gallatin</a>, Madison became of the two major influences in Jefferson's cabinet.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> At the start of his term, Madison was a party to the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Supreme_Court" class="mw-redirect" title="United States Supreme Court">United States Supreme Court</a> case <i><a href="/wiki/Marbury_v._Madison" title="Marbury v. Madison">Marbury v. Madison</a></i> (1803), in which the doctrine of <a href="/wiki/Judicial_review" title="Judicial review">judicial review</a> was asserted by the high Court, much to the annoyance of the Jeffersonians who did not want a powerful federalist judiciary.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Throughout Jefferson's presidency, much of Europe was at war, and the main challenge to the Jefferson administration was maintaining neutrality during the <a href="/wiki/Napoleonic_Wars" title="Napoleonic Wars">Napoleonic Wars</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup></p>
<p>Shortly before Jefferson's election, <a href="/wiki/Napoleon" title="Napoleon">Napoleon</a> seized power from the hapless <a href="/wiki/French_Directory" title="French Directory">French Directory</a>, which had recently mismanaged France's finances in unsuccessful wars and had lost control of <a href="/wiki/Saint-Domingue" title="Saint-Domingue">Saint-Domingue</a> (Haiti) after a slave rebellion. Beginning in 1802, Napoleon sent more than 20,000 troops in an attempt to restore slavery on the island, as its colonial sugar cane plantations had been the chief revenue producer for France in the New World. The warfare went badly and the troops were further decimated by <a href="/wiki/Yellow_fever" title="Yellow fever">yellow fever</a>. Napoleon gave up on the restoration of a colonial empire in the Americas and offered to sell the <a href="/wiki/Louisiana_(New_France)" title="Louisiana (New France)">Louisiana territory</a> to the United States in 1803. Later that year, the remaining 7,000 French troops were withdrawn from the island, and in 1804 <a href="/wiki/Haiti" title="Haiti">Haiti</a> declared its independence as the second republic in the western hemisphere.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Jefferson had originally sent delegates to France to negotiate the sale of <a href="/wiki/New_Orleans" title="New Orleans">New Orleans</a>, but upon learning of Napoleon's offer to sell the entire Louisiana territory, Jefferson quickly agreed to it, resulting in the <a href="/wiki/Louisiana_Purchase" title="Louisiana Purchase">Louisiana Purchase</a>. Many contemporaries and later historians, such as <a href="/wiki/Ron_Chernow" title="Ron Chernow">Ron Chernow</a>, noted that Madison and President Jefferson ignored their "strict construction" of the Constitution to take advantage of the purchase opportunity. Jefferson would have preferred a constitutional amendment authorizing the purchase, but did not have time nor was he required to do so. The Senate quickly ratified the treaty providing for the purchase. The House, with equal alacrity, passed enabling legislation.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> The Jefferson administration argued that the purchase had included <a href="/wiki/West_Florida" title="West Florida">West Florida</a>, but France refused to acknowledge this and Florida remained under the control of <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup></p>
<p>With the wars raging in Europe, Madison tried to maintain American neutrality, and insisted on the legal rights of the U.S. as a neutral party under international law. Neither London nor Paris showed much respect, however, and the situation deteriorated during Jefferson's second term. After Napoleon achieved victory at over his enemies in continental Europe at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Austerlitz" title="Battle of Austerlitz">Battle of Austerlitz</a>, he became more aggressive and tried to starve Britain into submission with an embargo that was economically ruinous to both sides. Madison and Jefferson also decided on an embargo to punish Britain and France, forbidding American trade with any foreign nation. The embargo failed in the United States just as it did in France, and caused massive hardships up and down the seaboard, which depended on foreign trade. The Federalists made a comeback in the Northeast by attacking the embargo, which was allowed to expire just as Jefferson was leaving office.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Election_of_1808">Election of 1808</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1808" title="United States presidential election, 1808">United States presidential election, 1808</a></div>
<p>With Jefferson's retirement imminent as his second term came to a close, Madison became his party's choice for president in 1808. He was opposed by Rep. <a href="/wiki/John_Randolph_of_Roanoke" title="John Randolph of Roanoke">John Randolph</a>, who had broken earlier with Jefferson and Madison. The Republican Party Congressional caucus chose the candidate and easily selected Madison over James Monroe.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> As the Federalist Party by this time had largely collapsed outside New England, Madison easily defeated Federalist <a href="/wiki/Charles_Cotesworth_Pinckney" title="Charles Cotesworth Pinckney">Charles Cotesworth Pinckney</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> At a height of only five feet, four inches (163&#160;cm), and never weighing more than 100 pounds, he became the most diminutive president.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Presidency_1809.E2.80.931817">Presidency 1809–1817</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Presidency_of_James_Madison" title="Presidency of James Madison">Presidency of James Madison</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:James_Madison_Portrait2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/James_Madison_Portrait2.jpg/170px-James_Madison_Portrait2.jpg" width="170" height="258" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/James_Madison_Portrait2.jpg/255px-James_Madison_Portrait2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/James_Madison_Portrait2.jpg/340px-James_Madison_Portrait2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="687" data-file-height="1044" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:James_Madison_Portrait2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
James Madison engraving by <a href="/wiki/David_Edwin" title="David Edwin">David Edwin</a> from between 1809 and 1817</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Upon his inauguration in 1809, Madison immediately faced opposition to his planned nomination of <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_the_Treasury" title="United States Secretary of the Treasury">Secretary of the Treasury</a> <a href="/wiki/Albert_Gallatin" title="Albert Gallatin">Albert Gallatin</a> as Secretary of State, led by Sen. <a href="/wiki/William_B._Giles" class="mw-redirect" title="William B. Giles">William B. Giles</a>. Madison chose not to fight Congress for the nomination but kept Gallatin, a carry over from the Jefferson administration, in the Treasury Department. The talented Swiss-born Gallatin was Madison's primary advisor, confidant, and policy planner.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> Madison appointed <a href="/wiki/Robert_Smith_(Cabinet_member)" title="Robert Smith (Cabinet member)">Robert Smith</a> for Secretary of State, Jefferson's former Secretary of the Navy. For his Secretary of the Navy, Madison appointed <a href="/wiki/Paul_Hamilton_(politician)" title="Paul Hamilton (politician)">Paul Hamilton</a>. Madison's cabinet, which included men of unremarkable talent, was chosen for the purposes of national interest and political harmony. When Madison assumed office in 1809, the federal government had a surplus of $9,500,000; by 1810 the national debt had been reduced, and taxes cut.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bank_of_the_United_States">Bank of the United States</span></h3>
<p>Madison sought to continue Jefferson's agenda—particularly dismantling the system left behind by the Federalist Party under Washington and Adams. One of the most pressing issues Madison confronted was the <a href="/wiki/First_Bank_of_the_United_States" title="First Bank of the United States">first Bank of the United States</a>. Its twenty-year charter was scheduled to expire in 1811, and while Gallatin insisted that the bank was a necessity, Congress failed to re-authorize it. As the absence of a national bank made war with Britain very difficult to finance, Congress passed a bill in 1814 chartering a second national bank. Madison vetoed it.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> In 1816, Congress passed another bill to charter a second national bank; Madison signed the act out of necessity during the war with Britain.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Prelude_to_war">Prelude to war</span></h3>
<p>Congress had repealed the embargo right before Madison became president, but troubles with the British and French continued.<sup id="cite_ref-Rp13_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rp13-89">[89]</a></sup> America's new <a href="/wiki/Non-Intercourse_Act_(1809)" title="Non-Intercourse Act (1809)">"nonintercourse" policy</a> was to trade with all countries including France and Britain if restrictions on shipping were removed.<sup id="cite_ref-Rp39_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rp39-90">[90]</a></sup> Although initially promising, Madison's diplomatic efforts to get the British to withdraw the <a href="/wiki/Orders_in_Council_(1807)" title="Orders in Council (1807)">Orders in Council</a> were rejected by British Foreign Secretary <a href="/wiki/George_Canning" title="George Canning">George Canning</a> in April 1809.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> Aside from U.S. trade with France, the central dispute between the Great Britain and the United States was the <a href="/wiki/Impressment" title="Impressment">impressment</a> of sailors by the British. During the long and expensive war against France, many British citizens were forced by their own government to join the navy, and many of these conscripts defected to U.S. merchant ships. Unable to tolerate this loss of manpower, the British seized several U.S. ships and forced captured crewmen, some of whom were not in fact not British subjects, to serve in the British navy. This impressment contributed greatly to growing anger towards the British in the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup></p>
<p>By August 1809, diplomatic relations with Britain deteriorated as minister <a href="/wiki/David_Erskine,_2nd_Baron_Erskine" title="David Erskine, 2nd Baron Erskine">David Erskine</a> was withdrawn and replaced by "hatchet man" <a href="/wiki/Francis_James_Jackson" title="Francis James Jackson">Francis James Jackson</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Rpp43-44_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rpp43-44-93">[93]</a></sup> Madison however, resisted calls for war, as he was ideologically opposed to the debt and taxes necessary for a war effort.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> British historian Paul Langford sees the removal in 1809 of Erskine as a major British blunder:</p>
<dl>
<dd>The British ambassador in Washington [Erskine] brought affairs almost to an accommodation, and was ultimately disappointed not by American intransigence but by one of the outstanding diplomatic blunders made by a Foreign Secretary. It was Canning who, in his most irresponsible manner and apparently out of sheer dislike of everything American, recalled the ambassador Erskine and wrecked the negotiations, a piece of most gratuitous folly. As a result, the possibility of a new embarrassment for Napoleon turned into the certainty of a much more serious one for his enemy. Though the British cabinet eventually made the necessary concessions on the score of the <a href="/wiki/Orders_in_Council_(1807)" title="Orders in Council (1807)">Orders-in-Council</a>, in response to the pressures of industrial lobbying at home, its action came too late…. The loss of the North American markets could have been a decisive blow. As it was by the time the United States declared war, the <a href="/wiki/Continental_System" title="Continental System">Continental System</a> [of Napoleon] was beginning to crack, and the danger correspondingly diminishing. Even so, the war, inconclusive though it proved in a military sense, was an irksome and expensive embarrassment which British statesman could have done much more to avert.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<p>After Jackson accused Madison of duplicity with Erskine, Madison had Jackson barred from the State Department and sent packing to Boston.<sup id="cite_ref-Rpp44-45_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rpp44-45-96">[96]</a></sup> During his first <a href="/wiki/State_of_the_Union_Address" class="mw-redirect" title="State of the Union Address">State of the Union Address</a> in November 1809, Madison asked Congress for advice and alternatives concerning the British-American trade crisis, and warned of the possibility of war. By spring 1810, Madison was specifically asking Congress for more appropriations to increase the Army and Navy in preparation for war with Britain.<sup id="cite_ref-Rp47_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rp47-97">[97]</a></sup> Together with the effects of European peace, the United States economy began to recover early in Madison's presidency. By the time Madison was standing for reelection, the <a href="/wiki/Peninsular_War" title="Peninsular War">Peninsular War</a> in Spain had spread, while at the same time Napoleon <a href="/wiki/Napoleon%27s_invasion_of_Russia" class="mw-redirect" title="Napoleon's invasion of Russia">invaded Russia</a>, and the entire European continent was once again embroiled in war.</p>
<p>By 1809 the Federalist Party was no longer competitive outside a few strongholds. Some former members (such as <a href="/wiki/John_Quincy_Adams" title="John Quincy Adams">John Quincy Adams</a>, Madison's ambassador to Russia) had joined Madison's Republican Party.<sup id="cite_ref-Rp55_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rp55-98">[98]</a></sup> Though one party appeared to dominate, it had begun to split into rival factions, which would later form the basis of the <a href="/wiki/Second_Party_System" title="Second Party System">Second Party System</a>. In particular, with hostilities against Britain appearing increasingly likely, factions favoring and opposing a war formed in Congress.<sup id="cite_ref-Rp57_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rp57-99">[99]</a></sup> The predominant faction, the "War Hawks," were led by <a href="/wiki/Speaker_of_the_United_States_House_of_Representatives" title="Speaker of the United States House of Representatives">House Speaker</a> <a href="/wiki/Henry_Clay" title="Henry Clay">Henry Clay</a>. When war finally did break out, the war effort was led by the War Hawks in Congress under Clay at least as much as it was by Madison; this accorded with the president's preference for checks and balances.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="War_of_1812">War of 1812</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/War_of_1812" title="War of 1812">War of 1812</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Battle_erie.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Battle_erie.jpg/300px-Battle_erie.jpg" width="300" height="186" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Battle_erie.jpg/450px-Battle_erie.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Battle_erie.jpg/600px-Battle_erie.jpg 2x" data-file-width="610" data-file-height="379" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Battle_erie.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Commodore <a href="/wiki/Oliver_Hazard_Perry" title="Oliver Hazard Perry">Oliver Hazard Perry</a> defeats British Navy at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Lake_Erie" title="Battle of Lake Erie">Battle of Lake Erie</a> in 1813.<br />
Powell <i>1873</i></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>With continued attacks by the British on American shipping, both Madison and the broader American public were ready for war with Britain.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> Many Americans called for a "second war of independence" to restore honor and stature to the new nation.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> With Britain in the midst of the Napoleonic Wars, many Americans, Madison included, believed that the United States could easily capture <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a>, at which point the U.S. could use Canada as a bargaining chip for all other disputes or simply retain control of it.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> An angry public elected a "war hawk" Congress, led by Clay and <a href="/wiki/John_C._Calhoun" title="John C. Calhoun">John C. Calhoun</a>. On June 1, 1812, Madison asked Congress for a declaration of war.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> The declaration was passed along sectional and party lines, with intense opposition from the Federalists and the Northeast, where the economy had suffered during Jefferson's trade embargo.<sup id="cite_ref-RRA_217-224_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RRA_217-224-104">[104]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup></p>
