<p>Madison hurriedly called on Congress to put the country "into an armor and an attitude demanded by the crisis," specifically recommending enlarging the army, preparing the militia, finishing the military academy, stockpiling munitions, and expanding the navy.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> Madison faced formidable obstacles—a divided cabinet, a factious party, a recalcitrant Congress, obstructionist governors, and incompetent generals, together with militia who refused to fight outside their states. The most serious problem facing the war effort was lack of unified popular support. There were serious threats of disunion from New England, which engaged in extensive smuggling with Canada and refused to provide financial support or soldiers.<sup id="cite_ref-stagg193_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stagg193-107">[107]</a></sup> Events in Europe also went against the United States. Shortly after the United States declared war, Napoleon launched an <a href="/wiki/French_invasion_of_Russia" title="French invasion of Russia">invasion of Russia</a>, and the failure of that campaign turned the tide against French and towards Britain and her allies.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup> In the years prior to the war, Jefferson and Madison had reduced the size of the military, closed the Bank of the U.S., and narrowed lowered taxes. These decisions added to the challenges facing the United States, as by the time the war began, Madison's military force consisted mostly of poorly trained militia members.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:USS_Constitution_vs_Guerriere.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/USS_Constitution_vs_Guerriere.jpg/220px-USS_Constitution_vs_Guerriere.jpg" width="220" height="151" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/USS_Constitution_vs_Guerriere.jpg/330px-USS_Constitution_vs_Guerriere.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/USS_Constitution_vs_Guerriere.jpg/440px-USS_Constitution_vs_Guerriere.jpg 2x" data-file-width="740" data-file-height="509" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:USS_Constitution_vs_Guerriere.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/USS_Constitution" title="USS Constitution">USS&#160;<i>Constitution</i></a> defeats <a href="/wiki/HMS_Guerriere_(1806)" title="HMS Guerriere (1806)">HMS <i>Guerriere</i></a>, a significant event during the war.</div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Military_action">Military action</span></h3>
<p>Madison hoped that the war would be over in a couple months after the capture of Canada, but his hopes were quickly dashed.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup> Madison had believed the state militias would rally to the flag and invade Canada, but the governors in the Northeast failed to cooperate. Their militias either sat out the war or refused to leave their respective states for action. The senior command at the War Department and in the field proved incompetent or cowardly—the general at Detroit surrendered to a smaller British force without firing a shot. Gallatin discovered the war was almost impossible to fund, since the national bank had been closed and major financiers in the Northeast refused to help.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup> The American campaign in Canada, led by <a href="/wiki/Henry_Dearborn" title="Henry Dearborn">Henry Dearborn</a>, ended with defeat in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Stoney_Creek" title="Battle of Stoney Creek">Battle of Stoney Creek</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup> The British armed American Indians in the Northwest, most notably several tribes allied with the <a href="/wiki/Shawnee" title="Shawnee">Shawnee</a> chief, <a href="/wiki/Tecumseh" title="Tecumseh">Tecumseh</a>. But, after losing control of Lake Erie at the naval <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Lake_Erie" title="Battle of Lake Erie">Battle of Lake Erie</a> in 1813, the British were forced to retreat. General <a href="/wiki/William_Henry_Harrison" title="William Henry Harrison">William Henry Harrison</a> caught up with them at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Thames" title="Battle of the Thames">Battle of the Thames</a>, where he destroyed the British and Indian armies, killed Tecumseh, and permanently destroyed Indian power in the Great Lakes region. Madison remains the only president to lead troops in battle while in office, although that battle (the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Bladensburg" title="Battle of Bladensburg">Battle of Bladensburg</a> in 1814) did not go well for the American side. The British then raided Washington, as Madison headed a dispirited militia. Dolley Madison rescued White House valuables and documents shortly before the British burned the White House, the Capitol and other public buildings.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup></p>
<p>After the disastrous start to the War of 1812, Madison accepted a Russian invitation to arbitrate the war and sent Gallatin, John Quincy Adams, and <a href="/wiki/James_A._Bayard_(elder)" title="James A. Bayard (elder)">James Bayard</a> to Europe in hopes of quickly ending the war.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> While Madison worked to end the war, the U.S. experienced some military success, particularly at sea. The U.S. naval squadron on <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Lake_Erie" title="Battle of Lake Erie">Lake Erie</a> successfully defended itself and captured its opponents of the Royal Navy. All six British vessels were captured by the American forces. This victory crippled the supply and reinforcement of British military forces in the western theatre of the war, which forced the British troops and their Native allies to retreat. Commander Oliver Hazard Perry reported his victory with the simple statement, "We have met the enemy, and they are ours."<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> America had built up one of the largest merchant fleets in the world, though it had been partially dismantled under Jefferson and Madison. Madison authorized many of these ships to become <a href="/wiki/Privateers" class="mw-redirect" title="Privateers">privateers</a> in the war. Armed, they captured 1,800 British ships.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup> As part of the war effort, an American naval shipyard was built up at <a href="/wiki/Sackets_Harbor,_New_York" title="Sackets Harbor, New York">Sackets Harbor, New York</a>, where thousands of men produced twelve warships and had another nearly ready by the end of the war. By 1814, generals <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Jackson" title="Andrew Jackson">Andrew Jackson</a> and William Henry Harrison had destroyed the main Indian threats in the South and West, respectively. In late 1814, Madison and his Secretary of War <a href="/wiki/James_Monroe" title="James Monroe">James Monroe</a> unsuccessfully asked Congress to establish a national draft of 40,000 men.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:256px;"><a href="/wiki/File:US_Capitol_1814c.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/US_Capitol_1814c.jpg/254px-US_Capitol_1814c.jpg" width="254" height="149" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/US_Capitol_1814c.jpg/381px-US_Capitol_1814c.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/US_Capitol_1814c.jpg/508px-US_Capitol_1814c.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4729" data-file-height="2774" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:US_Capitol_1814c.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The unfinished United States Capitol was set ablaze by the British on August 24, 1814.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The courageous, successful defense of <a href="/wiki/Ft._McHenry" class="mw-redirect" title="Ft. McHenry">Ft. McHenry</a>, which guarded the seaway to <a href="/wiki/Baltimore" title="Baltimore">Baltimore</a>, against one of the most intense naval bombardments in history (over 24 hours), led <a href="/wiki/Francis_Scott_Key" title="Francis Scott Key">Francis Scott Key</a> to write the poem that was set to music as the U.S. national anthem, "<a href="/wiki/The_Star_Spangled_Banner" class="mw-redirect" title="The Star Spangled Banner">The Star Spangled Banner</a>."<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> U.S. victory at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Plattsburgh" title="Battle of Plattsburgh">Battle of Plattsburgh</a> ended British hopes of conquering New York.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup> In New Orleans, Gen. Andrew Jackson put together a force including regular Army troops, militia, frontiersmen, Creoles, Native American allies and <a href="/wiki/Jean_Lafitte" title="Jean Lafitte">Jean Lafitte</a>'s pirates. The <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_New_Orleans" title="Battle of New Orleans">Battle of New Orleans</a> took place two weeks after peace treaty was drafted (but before it was ratified, so the war was not over). The American defenders repulsed the British invasion army in the most decisive victory of the war.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Ghent" title="Treaty of Ghent">Treaty of Ghent</a> ended the war in February 1815, with no territorial gains on either side. The Americans felt that their national honor had been restored in what has been called "the Second War of American Independence."<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup> On March 3, 1815, the U.S. Congress authorized deployment of naval power against Algiers, and two squadrons were assembled and readied for war; the <a href="/wiki/Second_Barbary_War" title="Second Barbary War">Second Barbary War</a> would mark the beginning of the end for piracy in that region.</p>
<p>To most Americans, the quick succession of events at the end of the war (the burning of the capital, the Battle of New Orleans, and the Treaty of Ghent) appeared as though American valor at New Orleans had forced the British to surrender after almost winning. This view, while inaccurate, strongly contributed to the post-war euphoria that persisted for a decade. It also helps explain the significance of the war, even if it was strategically inconclusive. Napoleon was defeated for the last time at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Waterloo" title="Battle of Waterloo">Battle of Waterloo</a> near the end of Madison's presidency, and as the Napoleonic Wars ended, so did the War of 1812. Madison's final years began an unprecedented period of peace and prosperity, which was called the <a href="/wiki/Era_of_Good_Feelings" title="Era of Good Feelings">Era of Good Feelings</a>. Madison's reputation as president improved and Americans finally believed the United States had established itself as a world power.<sup id="cite_ref-Rp188_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rp188-122">[122]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Postwar_economy_and_internal_improvements">Postwar economy and internal improvements</span></h3>
<p>With peace established, Americans believed they had an emboldened independence from Britain. The Federalist Party, which had called for secession over the war at the <a href="/wiki/Hartford_Convention" title="Hartford Convention">Hartford Convention</a>, dissolved. With Europe finally at peace, the <a href="/wiki/Era_of_Good_Feelings" title="Era of Good Feelings">Era of Good Feelings</a> described the prosperity and equable political environment. Some political contention continued; for instance, in 1816, two-thirds of the incumbents in Congress were defeated for re-election after voting to increase their salary. Madison approved a Hamiltonian national bank, an effective taxation system based on tariffs, a standing professional military, and the internal improvements championed by <a href="/wiki/Henry_Clay" title="Henry Clay">Henry Clay</a> under his <a href="/wiki/American_System_(economic_plan)" title="American System (economic plan)">American System</a>. In 1816, pensions were extended to orphans and widows from the War of 1812 for a period of 5 years at the rate of half pay.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup></p>
<p>However, in his last act before leaving office, Madison vetoed the <a href="/wiki/Bonus_Bill_of_1817" title="Bonus Bill of 1817">Bonus Bill of 1817</a>, which would have financed more internal improvements of roads, bridges, and canals: "Having considered the bill this day presented to me&#160;... I am constrained by the insuperable difficulty I feel in reconciling this bill with the Constitution of the United States.... The legislative powers vested in Congress are specified and enumerated in&#160;... the Constitution, and it does not appear that the power proposed to be exercised by the bill is among the enumerated powers."<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup> Madison rejected the view of Congress that the General Welfare provision of the <a href="/wiki/Taxing_and_Spending_Clause" title="Taxing and Spending Clause">Taxing and Spending Clause</a> justified the bill: "Such a view of the Constitution would have the effect of giving to Congress a general power of legislation instead of the defined and limited one hitherto understood to belong to them, the terms 'common defense and general welfare' embracing every object and act within the purview of a legislative trust." Madison urged a variety of measures that he felt were "best executed under the national authority," including federal support for roads and canals that would "bind more closely together the various parts of our extended confederacy."<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Indian_policy">Indian policy</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Benjamin_Hawkins_and_the_Creek_Indians.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Benjamin_Hawkins_and_the_Creek_Indians.jpg/220px-Benjamin_Hawkins_and_the_Creek_Indians.jpg" width="220" height="144" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Benjamin_Hawkins_and_the_Creek_Indians.jpg/330px-Benjamin_Hawkins_and_the_Creek_Indians.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Benjamin_Hawkins_and_the_Creek_Indians.jpg/440px-Benjamin_Hawkins_and_the_Creek_Indians.jpg 2x" data-file-width="528" data-file-height="345" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Benjamin_Hawkins_and_the_Creek_Indians.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Muscogee_(Creek)" class="mw-redirect" title="Muscogee (Creek)">Creek</a> men being taught how to use a plow by <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Hawkins" title="Benjamin Hawkins">Benjamin Hawkins</a> in 1805.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Upon assuming office on March 4, 1809, in his first <a href="/wiki/Inaugural_Address" class="mw-redirect" title="Inaugural Address">Inaugural Address</a> to the nation, Madison stated that the federal government's duty was to convert the American Indians by the "participation of the improvements of which the human mind and manners are susceptible in a civilized state".<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup> Like Jefferson, Madison had a paternalistic attitude toward American Indians, encouraging the men to give up hunting and become farmers.<sup id="cite_ref-Rp37_127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rp37-127">[127]</a></sup> Although there are scant details, Madison often met with Southeastern and Western Indians who included the <a href="/wiki/Muscogee_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Muscogee people">Creek</a> and <a href="/wiki/Osage_Nation" title="Osage Nation">Osage</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Rp37_127-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rp37-127">[127]</a></sup> Madison believed their adoption of European-style agriculture would help the Creek assimilate the values of British-American civilization. As pioneers and settlers moved West into large tracts of <a href="/wiki/Cherokee" title="Cherokee">Cherokee</a>, <a href="/wiki/Choctaw" title="Choctaw">Choctaw</a>, <a href="/wiki/Muscogee_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Muscogee people">Creek</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Chickasaw" title="Chickasaw">Chickasaw</a> territory, Madison ordered the US Army to protect Native lands from intrusion by settlers, to the chagrin of his military commander <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Jackson" title="Andrew Jackson">Andrew Jackson</a>. Jackson wanted the President to ignore Indian pleas to stop the invasion of their lands<sup id="cite_ref-Rpp199-200_128-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rpp199-200-128">[128]</a></sup> and resisted carrying out the president's order.<sup id="cite_ref-Rpp199-200_128-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rpp199-200-128">[128]</a></sup> In the Northwest Territory after the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Tippecanoe" title="Battle of Tippecanoe">Battle of Tippecanoe</a> in 1811, Indians were pushed off their tribal lands and replaced entirely by white settlers.<sup id="cite_ref-Rpp199-200_128-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rpp199-200-128">[128]</a></sup> By 1815, with a population of 400,000 European-American settlers in Ohio, Indian rights to their lands had effectively become <a href="/wiki/Null_and_void" class="mw-redirect" title="Null and void">null and void</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Rpp199-200_128-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rpp199-200-128">[128]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Later_life">Later life</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:226px;"><a href="/wiki/File:JamesMadison.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/JamesMadison.jpg/224px-JamesMadison.jpg" width="224" height="265" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/JamesMadison.jpg/336px-JamesMadison.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/JamesMadison.jpg/448px-JamesMadison.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2433" data-file-height="2873" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:JamesMadison.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<center><i>Portrait of James Madison</i> c. 1821,<br />
by <a href="/wiki/Gilbert_Stuart" title="Gilbert Stuart">Gilbert Stuart</a></center>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>When Madison left office in 1817 at age 65, he retired to <a href="/wiki/Montpelier_(James_Madison)" class="mw-redirect" title="Montpelier (James Madison)">Montpelier</a>, his tobacco plantation in <a href="/wiki/Orange_County,_Virginia" title="Orange County, Virginia">Orange County, Virginia</a>, not far from Jefferson's <a href="/wiki/Monticello" title="Monticello">Monticello</a>. Dolley, who thought they would finally have a chance to travel to Paris, was 49. As with both Washington and Jefferson, Madison left the presidency a poorer man than when elected. His plantation experienced a steady financial collapse, due to the continued price declines in tobacco and also due to his stepson's mismanagement.</p>
<p>Insight into Madison is provided by the first "White House memoir, <i>A Colored Man's Reminiscences of James Madison</i> (1865), authored by his former slave <a href="/wiki/Paul_Jennings_(slave)" title="Paul Jennings (slave)">Paul Jennings</a>, who from the age of 10 served the president as a footman, and later as a valet for the rest of Madison's life. After Madison's death, Jennings was purchased in 1845 from Dolley Madison by senator <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Webster" title="Daniel Webster">Daniel Webster</a>, who enabled him to work off the cost and gain his freedom. Jennings published his short account in 1865.<sup id="cite_ref-reminiscences_129-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-reminiscences-129">[129]</a></sup> He had the highest respect for Madison and said he never struck a slave, nor permitted an overseer to do so. Jennings said that if a slave misbehaved, Madison would meet with the person privately to discuss the behavior.<sup id="cite_ref-reminiscences_129-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-reminiscences-129">[129]</a></sup></p>
<p>Some historians speculate that Madison's mounting debt was one of the reasons he refused to allow his notes on the Constitutional Convention, or its official records in his possession, to be published in his lifetime. "He knew the value of his notes, and wanted them to bring money to his estate for Dolley's use as his plantation failed—he was hoping for one hundred thousand dollars from the sale of his papers, of which the notes were the gem."<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup> Madison's financial troubles weighed on him as deteriorating mental and physical health ensued.</p>
<p>In his later years, Madison became highly concerned about his historic legacy. He resorted to modifying letters and other documents in his possession, changing days and dates, adding and deleting words and sentences, and shifting characters. By the time he had reached his late seventies, this "straightening out" had become almost an obsession. As an example, he edited a letter written to Jefferson criticizing Lafayette—Madison not only inked out original passages, but even forged Jefferson's handwriting as well.<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup> Historian <a href="/wiki/Drew_R._McCoy" title="Drew R. McCoy">Drew R. McCoy</a> has said, "During the final six years of his life, amid a sea of personal [financial] troubles that were threatening to engulf him...At times mental agitation issued in physical collapse. For the better part of a year in 1831 and 1832 he was bedridden, if not silenced... Literally sick with anxiety, he began to despair of his ability to make himself understood by his fellow citizens."<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1826, after the death of Jefferson, Madison was appointed as the second Rector ("President") of the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Virginia" title="University of Virginia">University of Virginia</a>. He retained the position as college chancellor for ten years until his death in 1836.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:226px;"><a href="/wiki/File:James_madison-Age82-Edit1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/James_madison-Age82-Edit1.jpg/224px-James_madison-Age82-Edit1.jpg" width="224" height="249" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/James_madison-Age82-Edit1.jpg/336px-James_madison-Age82-Edit1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/James_madison-Age82-Edit1.jpg/448px-James_madison-Age82-Edit1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1812" data-file-height="2014" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:James_madison-Age82-Edit1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<center>Portrait of Madison, age 82,<br />
c. 1833</center>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1829, at the age of 78, Madison was chosen as a representative to the <a href="/wiki/Virginia_Constitutional_Convention_of_1829%E2%80%931830" title="Virginia Constitutional Convention of 1829–1830">Virginia Constitutional Convention</a> for revision of the commonwealth's constitution. It was his last appearance as a statesman. The issue of greatest importance at this convention was <a href="/wiki/Apportionment_(politics)" title="Apportionment (politics)">apportionment</a>. The western districts of Virginia complained that they were underrepresented because the state constitution apportioned voting districts by county. The increased population in the Piedmont and western parts of the state were not proportionately represented by delegates in the legislature. Western reformers also wanted to extend suffrage to all white men, in place of the prevailing property ownership requirement. Madison tried in vain to effect a compromise. Eventually, suffrage rights were extended to renters as well as landowners, but the eastern planters refused to adopt citizen population apportionment. They added slaves held as property to the population count, to maintain a permanent majority in both houses of the legislature, arguing that there must be a balance between population and property represented. Madison was disappointed at the failure of Virginians to resolve the issue more equitably.<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup></p>
<p>Madison was also concerned about the continuation of slavery in Virginia and the South. He believed that transportation of free American blacks to Africa offered a solution, as promoted by the <a href="/wiki/American_Colonization_Society" title="American Colonization Society">American Colonization Society</a> (ACS).<sup id="cite_ref-Cp138_134-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cp138-134">[134]</a></sup> He told <a href="/wiki/Gilbert_du_Motier,_marquis_de_Lafayette" class="mw-redirect" title="Gilbert du Motier, marquis de Lafayette">Lafayette</a> at the time of the convention that colonization would create a "rapid erasure of the blot on our Republican character."<sup id="cite_ref-McCoy_135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McCoy-135">[135]</a></sup> The British sociologist <a href="/wiki/Harriet_Martineau" title="Harriet Martineau">Harriet Martineau</a> visited with Madison during her tour of the United States in 1834. She characterized his belief in the colonization solution to slavery as "bizarre and incongruous."<sup id="cite_ref-McCoy_135-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McCoy-135">[135]</a></sup> Madison may have sold or donated his <a href="/wiki/Gristmill" title="Gristmill">gristmill</a> in support of the ACS.<sup id="cite_ref-Cp138_134-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cp138-134">[134]</a></sup> Historian McCoy believes that "The Convention of 1829... pushed Madison steadily to the brink of self-delusion, if not despair. The dilemma of slavery undid him."<sup id="cite_ref-McCoy_135-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McCoy-135">[135]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup> Like most African Americans of the time, Madison's slaves wanted to remain in the U.S. where they had been born, believed their work earned them citizenship, and resisted "repatriation".<sup id="cite_ref-Cp138_134-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cp138-134">[134]</a></sup></p>
<p>William R. Denslow supposedly found evidence that James Madison might have been a Mason,<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup> when in 1795, <a href="/wiki/John_Francis_Mercer" title="John Francis Mercer">John Francis Mercer</a> is said to have written to Madison, "I have had no opportunity of congratulating you before on your becoming a free Mason—a very ancient &amp; honorable fraternity."<sup id="cite_ref-nationalarchives-mercer-madison_138-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nationalarchives-mercer-madison-138">[138]</a></sup> However, in an 1832 letter to Stephen Bates, Madison wrote that he never was a Mason, and was a "stranger to the principles" of it.<sup id="cite_ref-fea-notamason_139-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fea-notamason-139">[139]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:James_Madison_gravestone_IMG_4299.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/James_Madison_gravestone_IMG_4299.JPG/220px-James_Madison_gravestone_IMG_4299.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/James_Madison_gravestone_IMG_4299.JPG/330px-James_Madison_gravestone_IMG_4299.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/James_Madison_gravestone_IMG_4299.JPG/440px-James_Madison_gravestone_IMG_4299.JPG 2x" data-file-width="4320" data-file-height="3240" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:James_Madison_gravestone_IMG_4299.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Madison's tombstone, Montpelier</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Despite failing health, Madison wrote several memoranda on political subjects, including an essay against the appointment of <a href="/wiki/Chaplain" title="Chaplain">chaplains</a> for Congress and the armed forces. He felt it would produce religious exclusion as well as political disharmony. He was tempted to admit chaplains for the navy, as sailors might otherwise have no opportunity for worship.<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup></p>
<p><span id="Death"></span> Between 1834 and 1835, he sold 25% of his slaves to offset financial losses on his plantation.<sup id="cite_ref-Cp138_134-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cp138-134">[134]</a></sup> Madison lived until 1836, increasingly disregarded by the next generation of the American polity. He died of heart failure at Montpelier on June 28. He was buried in the Madison Family Cemetery at Montpelier.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1842, Dolley Madison sold the Montpelier mansion, and in 1844 sold the extensive plantation lands to Henry W. Moncure.<sup id="cite_ref-Cp138_134-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cp138-134">[134]</a></sup> She leased half of the remaining slaves to Moncure. The other half were inherited by her, her son <a href="/wiki/John_Payne_Todd" title="John Payne Todd">John Payne Todd</a>, and James Madison, Jr., a nephew.<sup id="cite_ref-Cp138_134-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cp138-134">[134]</a></sup> By 1850, the Montpelier plantation was a "ghost of its former self".<sup id="cite_ref-Cp138_134-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cp138-134">[134]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">Legacy</span></h2>
<p>The historian <a href="/wiki/Garry_Wills" title="Garry Wills">Garry Wills</a> wrote, "Madison's claim on our admiration does not rest on a perfect consistency, any more than it rests on his presidency. He has other virtues. ... As a framer and defender of the Constitution he had no peer. ... The finest part of Madison's performance as president was his concern for the preserving of the Constitution. ... No man could do everything for the country—not even Washington. Madison did more than most, and did some things better than any. That was quite enough."<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/George_F._Will" class="mw-redirect" title="George F. Will">George F. Will</a> wrote that if we truly believed that the pen is mightier than the sword, our nation's capital would have been called "Madison, D.C.", instead of Washington, D.C.<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup></p>
<p>Madison, in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Federalist_Papers" title="The Federalist Papers">The Federalist Papers</a>,</i> in <a href="/wiki/Federalist_No._51" title="Federalist No. 51">Federalist No. 51</a>, wrote, "It is of great importance in a republic not only to guard the society against the oppression of its rulers, but to guard one part of the society against the injustice of the other part... In a society under the forms of which the stronger faction can readily unite and oppress the weaker, anarchy may as truly be said to reign as in a state of nature, where the weaker individual is not secured against the violence of the stronger."</p>
<p>In 1986, Congress created the <a href="/wiki/James_Madison_Memorial_Fellowship_Foundation" title="James Madison Memorial Fellowship Foundation">James Madison Memorial Fellowship Foundation</a> as part of the bicentennial celebration of the Constitution. The Foundation offers $24,000 graduate level fellowships to secondary teachers to undertake a master's degree which emphasizes the study of the Constitution.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Montpelier_(Orange,_Virginia)" title="Montpelier (Orange, Virginia)">Montpelier</a>, his family's plantation and his home in Orange, Virginia, has been designated a <a href="/wiki/National_Historic_Landmark" title="National Historic Landmark">National Historic Landmark</a>.</p>
<p>Many counties, several towns, cities, educational institutions, a mountain range and a river are named after Madison:</p>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Madison_County_(disambiguation)" class="mw-redirect mw-disambig" title="Madison County (disambiguation)">Madison County</a> – lists counties named for him</li>
<li>Cities: e.g. <a href="/wiki/Madison,_Wisconsin" title="Madison, Wisconsin">Madison, Wisconsin</a></li>
<li>Named in his honor were the <a href="/wiki/James_Madison_College" title="James Madison College">James Madison College</a> of public policy at <a href="/wiki/Michigan_State_University" title="Michigan State University">Michigan State University</a>; and <a href="/wiki/James_Madison_University" title="James Madison University">James Madison University</a> in <a href="/wiki/Harrisonburg,_Virginia" title="Harrisonburg, Virginia">Harrisonburg, Virginia</a>; the <a href="/wiki/James_Madison_Institute" title="James Madison Institute">James Madison Institute</a> honors his contributions to the Constitution.</li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/James_Madison_Memorial_Building" title="James Madison Memorial Building">James Madison Memorial Building</a> is a building of the United States <a href="/wiki/Library_of_Congress" title="Library of Congress">Library of Congress</a> in <a href="/wiki/Washington,_DC" class="mw-redirect" title="Washington, DC">Washington, DC</a> and serves as the official memorial to Madison.</li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Madison_Range" title="Madison Range">Madison Range</a> was named in honor of the then U.S. Secretary of State by <a href="/wiki/Meriwether_Lewis" title="Meriwether Lewis">Meriwether Lewis</a> as the <a href="/wiki/Lewis_and_Clark_Expedition" title="Lewis and Clark Expedition">Lewis and Clark Expedition</a> traveled through Montana in 1805. The <a href="/wiki/Madison_River" title="Madison River">Madison River</a> in southwestern Montana, was named in 1805 by Lewis &amp; Clark.<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Mount_Madison" title="Mount Madison">Mount Madison</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Presidential_Range" title="Presidential Range">Presidential Range</a> of the White Mountains in New Hampshire is named for him.</li>
<li>Two <a href="/wiki/U.S._Navy" class="mw-redirect" title="U.S. Navy">U.S. Navy</a> ships have been named <a href="/wiki/USS_James_Madison" title="USS James Madison">USS <i>James Madison</i></a> and three as <a href="/wiki/USS_Madison" title="USS Madison">USS <i>Madison</i></a>.</li>
<li>Madison's portrait was on the old U.S. <a href="/wiki/Large_denominations_of_United_States_currency" title="Large denominations of United States currency">$5000 bill</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Madison_Square" title="Madison Square">Madison Cottage</a> in New York City was named in his honor shortly after his death. It had been an important way station at the northernmost edge of New York City. The grounds where the cottage was located became <a href="/wiki/Madison_Square" title="Madison Square">Madison Square</a> park. The park in turn gave rise to the name for <a href="/wiki/Madison_Square_Gardens" class="mw-redirect" title="Madison Square Gardens">Madison Square Gardens</a> which was originally located adjacent to the park. Madison Square Gardens was an important bicycle racing venue and the subsequent host of a type of cycling known as <a href="/wiki/Madison_(cycling)" title="Madison (cycling)">Madison Cycling</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<p>Madison is also a character depicted in 2015 musical <i><a href="/wiki/Hamilton_(musical)" title="Hamilton (musical)">Hamilton</a></i>, originated by actor <a href="/wiki/Okieriete_Onaodowan" title="Okieriete Onaodowan">Okieriete Onaodowan</a> both off- and on-Broadway; Madison is portrayed as an ally to <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson" title="Thomas Jefferson">Thomas Jefferson</a> and adversary to <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Hamilton" title="Alexander Hamilton">Alexander Hamilton</a> in the second act of the show.</p>
